(۳) قوله : (موصوفاً) أي : مسمّى بأسمائه انتهى أجهوري.
And it was investigated in this answer (1) that the naming is the placing of the name for the named, and since the name is originated, the naming is originated?
(1) His saying: (And it was investigated in this answer) – this investigation is not apparent; for we say: the naming is the placing of the name, and it is Allah's eternal intention that the utterances existing in His knowledge indicate Him in the future, and then the origination of the name does not necessitate the origination of its placing; because the placing of the name does not depend on uttering it. – End of al-Ujhuri.
Tuhfat al-Mureed on Jawharat al-Tawhid
And it was answered that the meaning of its eternity is that Allah is worthy of it eternally, so it is eternal in terms of worthiness.
(1)
But it is objected that this does not avail against the Muʿtazila who say that they are from the creation of creatures, since it does not contradict it. And some answered that its eternity is by virtue of Allah's knowledge and His predestination in pre‑eternity, but then all originated things are likewise. And it was said that its eternity is by virtue of its meaning, but then the eternity of the meaning goes back to what preceded of the eternity of the essence and attributes, and it does not avail against the Muʿtazila. And al‑Mallawi transmitted from Sayyidī Muḥammad ibn ʿAbd Allāh al‑ʿArabī: in what preceded, that from Allah's eternal speech are names for Him that are judged to be eternal, just as from it are command and prohibition ... etc.), and upon this, the intended meaning of the eternal naming is the indication of speech eternally to the meanings of the names without division or separation in speech. This is what expands the chest, and it is not refuted that they did not mention among the intellectual divisions of speech the eternal names; because their division is not exhaustive, rather they restricted themselves to the most important according to what appeared to them. How can it be when its meaning cannot be confined?!
T
Al‑awwaliyyah
And al‑Mallawi indicated at the end of his statement that eternity here is not in the sense of lack of beginning, but in the sense that they are placed before creation, so they are from His placing before His creation, then He inspired them to the Muhammadan Light, then to the angels, then to the creatures, contrary to the Muʿtazila in their statement that they are from the placement of humans. In this speech there is an admission that the names are not eternal, as is not hidden.
Ha. (2)
In summary: This topic is not clear.
And it was transmitted from al‑Qurṭubī: that whoever says the name is derived from al‑sumuww (highness) says: Allah has never ceased to be described (3) before the existence of creatures, at their existence, and after their annihilation – no effect on His names from them. And this is the statement of Ahl al‑Sunnah.
In it, and whoever says the name is derived from al‑simah (mark) says: He was in pre‑eternity without names.
(1) His saying: (in what preceded) i.e., in what preceded of his transmission from them, which is that His names are originated and that they are from the creation of creatures. – End of al‑Ujhuri.
(2) His saying: (So this topic is not clear) – built upon the investigation of the first answer, which is that they are eternal by virtue of the naming, and you have known from what was mentioned in the margin that it is invalid. – End of al‑Ujhuri.
(3) His saying: (described) i.e., named by His names. – End of al‑Ujhuri.
Source pages p. 202

Commentary
This passage is a continuous discussion of objections and further answers concerning the eternity of Allah's names. It begins by critically examining the first answer (that names are eternal by virtue of the naming). The critique argues that if the name is originated, then the naming act (taṣmiya) is also originated. A marginal note from al‑Ujhuri counters that the naming is Allah's eternal intention, not dependent on uttering. Then the main text presents an alternative answer: the names are eternal because Allah is worthy of them (ṣāliḥ) eternally. However, this is deemed insufficient against the Muʿtazila because it does not refute their claim that names are created by humans. Another answer says eternity is by virtue of Allah's knowledge in pre‑eternity, but this would apply to all created things. Another says it is by virtue of the meaning (madlūl), but that goes back to the eternity of the essence and attributes. The commentator then transmits from al‑Mallawi that the names are part of Allah's eternal speech (kalām), as command and prohibition are, and that the eternal naming is the eternal indication of speech to the meanings of the names without division. This is praised as satisfying. Al‑Mallawi further clarifies that 'eternity' here means they are placed before creation, not that they are without beginning. Finally, two etymologies of 'ism' (name) are presented: from sumuww (highness) implying eternal description, and from simah (mark) implying that in pre‑eternity there were no names. The first is the view of Ahl al‑Sunnah.

Key Terms
التسمية
الصلاحية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that any answer about the eternity of names is equally valid.
Correction: The passage shows that the worthiness answer is insufficient against the Mu'tazila; a stronger answer is needed.

Checkpoint
Why is the worthiness answer considered insufficient against the Mu'tazila?
A) It does not explain how names can be eternal.
B) It does not contradict the Mu'tazila claim that names are created by humans.
C) It relies on a false etymology.
D) It implies names are not truly eternal.

Key Takeaway
The worthiness answer redefines the eternity of names but is insufficient against the Mu'tazila because it does not refute their claim.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The worthiness answer seemed like a solid defense, yet it failed to refute the Mu'tazila. In what areas of your own faith do you rely on arguments that feel convincing but might not withstand scrutiny? What will you do to ensure your beliefs are grounded in revelation, not just clever reasoning?
?
You call upon Allah by His names daily. Have you ever considered the possibility that these names might be human conventions, as the Mu'tazila claim? If that thought unsettles you, what concrete evidence from the Qur'an and Sunnah can you produce to affirm their eternity? Commit to finding and memorizing one proof today.

intermediate
4 min
Further Answers: Knowledge, Meaning, and the Eternal Speech View
Examination of knowledge and meaning answers, then presentation of the eternal speech answer.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the alternative answers that the eternity of names is based on Allah's knowledge or the meaning of names, and the transition to the preferred answer that names are part of Allah's eternal speech.

Core Idea
Names are eternal because they are part of Allah's eternal speech, not merely because of knowledge or meaning.

The text continues to explore answers to the problem of the eternity of names. Some say they are eternal by virtue of Allah's knowledge and predestination, but this applies to all created things. Others say by virtue of their meaning, but that reduces to the eternity of attributes. Finally, al-Mallawi transmits a view that names are part of Allah's eternal speech, which is more satisfying.

Guided Path
1
Read the knowledge-based answer
Note that the answer claims names are eternal because Allah knew them and predestined them in pre-eternity.
The critique is that this applies to all created things, not just names.
2
Read the meaning-based answer
The answer claims names are eternal because their meanings (madlul) are eternal, but the critique is that this reduces to the eternity of the essence and attributes.
3
Read the preferred answer from al-Mallawi
Al-Mallawi transmits that names are part of Allah's eternal speech (kalam), like command and prohibition. Thus, the eternal naming is the eternal indication of speech to the meanings of names without division.
This is presented as the satisfying answer.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وبُحث في هذا الجواب (۱) بأن التسمية وضع الاسم للمسمّى، وحيث كان الاسم حادثاً كانت التسمية حادثة؟
(۱) قوله : ( وبحث هذا الجواب هذا البحث غير ظاهر ؛ لأنا نقول : التسمية هي وضع الاسم، وهو قصد الله أزلاً أن تكون الألفاظ الموجودة في علمه دالّة عليه فيما لا يزال، وحينئذ لا يلزم من حدوث الاسم حدوث وضعه ؛ لأن وضع الاسم لا يتوقف على النطق به. انتهى أجهوري.
تحفة المريد على جوهرة التوحيد
وأجيب بأنَّ معنى قدمها : أنَّ الله صالح لها ،ازلاً، فهي قديمة باعتبار الصلاحية.
(1)
وفيه أن هذا لا يحسن في الردّ على المعتزلة الذين يقولون : إنَّها من وضع الخلق، إذ لا ينافيه وبعضهم أجاب بأن قدمها من حيث علم الله تعالى وتقديره في الأزل، وفيه أن جميع الحوادث كذلك، وقيل: إنَّ قدمها من حيث مدلولها ، وفيه أنَّ قدم المدلول يرجع لما سبق من قدم الذات والصفات ولا يحسنُ في الرد على المعتزلة ونقل العلامة الملوي عن سيدي محمد بن عبد الله العربي : فيما سبق أنَّ من كلام الله القديم أسماء له هي المحكوم عليها بالقدم، كما أن منه أمراً ونهياً ... إلى آخره)، وعلى هذا فالمراد بالتسمية القديمة دلالة الكلام أزلاً على معاني الأسماء من غير تبعيض ولا تجزئة في الكلام، وهو الذي ينشرح له الصدر، ولا يردُ أنَّهم لم يذكروا من أقسام الكلام الاعتبارية الأسماء القديمة؛ لأنَّ تقسيمهم ليس حاصراً، بل اقتصروا على الأهم باعتبار ما ظهر لهم كيف ومدلوله لا يدخل تحت حصر ؟!

الأولية
وأشار العلامة الملوي في آخر عبارته إلى أن القدم هنا ليس بمعنى عدم بل بمعنى أنَّها موضوعةُ قبل الخلق، فهي من وضعه تعالى قبل خلقه، ثم ألهمها للنور المحمدي، ثم للملائكة، ثم للخلق، خلافاً للمعتزلة في قولهم : بأنَّها من وضع البشر، وفي هذا الكلام تسليمُ أنَّ الأسماء ليست أزليَّةٌ كما لا يخفى.

وبالجملة : فهذا المبحث لم يَصْفُ
ونُقل عن القرطبي : أنَّ مَنْ قال: الاسم مشتق من السمو وهو العلو، يقول: لم يزل الله موصوفاً (۳) قبل وجود الخلق وعند وجودهم وبعد فنائهم لا تأثير لهم أسمائه، وهذا قول أهل السنة.

ومن قال : الاسم مشتق من السمة، يقول: كان في الأزل بلا أسماء

(۲) قوله : (فهذا البحث لم يصفُ) مبني على البحث في الجواب الأول ؛ وهو أنها قديمة باعتبار التسمية، وقد علمت مما ذكر في الهامش أنه غير وارد. انتهى أجهوري.
(۳) قوله : (موصوفاً) أي : مسمّى بأسمائه انتهى أجهوري.
And it was investigated in this answer (1) that the naming is the placing of the name for the named, and since the name is originated, the naming is originated?
(1) His saying: (And it was investigated in this answer) – this investigation is not apparent; for we say: the naming is the placing of the name, and it is Allah's eternal intention that the utterances existing in His knowledge indicate Him in the future, and then the origination of the name does not necessitate the origination of its placing; because the placing of the name does not depend on uttering it. – End of al-Ujhuri.

According to al-Mallawi, what is the 'eternal naming'?
A) The act of naming in pre-eternity

?

?

?

The Meaning of 'Eternity' and the Two Etymologies of 'Ism'
Clarification of the meaning of 'eternity' and the two etymologies of 'ism'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that 'eternity' here means pre-creation placement, not absolute uncreatedness, and the two etymologies of 'ism' leading to different views on whether Allah had names in pre-eternity.

Core Idea
Names are 'eternal' in the sense of being placed before creation, and the etymology of 'ism' determines whether Allah is named eternally.

This passage clarifies the meaning of 'eternity' (qidam) in this context: it does not mean uncreatedness in time, but rather that the names were placed by Allah before creation. Then it presents two etymologies of the word 'ism' (name): from 'sumuww' (highness) implying eternal description, and from 'simah' (mark) implying that in pre-eternity there were no names. The first is claimed as the view of Ahl al-Sunnah.

Guided Path
1
Read al-Mallawi's clarification of 'eternity'
Note that 'eternity' here is not the usual philosophical meaning of lacking a beginning, but rather 'placed before creation'.
This implies the names are not absolutely eternal in the sense of being without beginning.
2
Read the two etymologies of 'ism'
The first etymology from 'sumuww' (highness) leads to the view that Allah has always been described by His names. The second from 'simah' (mark) leads to the view that in pre-eternity there were no names.
The author states the first is the view of Ahl al-Sunnah.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Another says it is by virtue of the meaning (madlūl), but that goes back to the eternity of the essence and attributes. The commentator then transmits from al‑Mallawi that the names are part of Allah's eternal speech (kalām), as command and prohibition are, and that the eternal naming is the eternal indication of speech to the meanings of the names without division. This is praised as satisfying. Al‑Mallawi further clarifies that 'eternity' here means they are placed before creation, not that they are without beginning. Finally, two etymologies of 'ism' (name) are presented: from sumuww (highness) implying eternal description, and from simah (mark) implying that in pre‑eternity there were no names. The first is the view of Ahl al‑Sunnah.

Key Terms
الاسم
السمو
السمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think 'eternal' in this context means 'uncreated' in the absolute sense.
Correction: Al-Mallawi clarifies that it means 'placed before creation', not absolutely beginningless.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the view of Ahl al-Sunnah regarding the derivation of 'ism'?
A) It is derived from simah, so Allah had no names in pre-eternity.
B) It is derived from sumuww, so Allah has always been described by His names.
C) It is derived from sumuww, so names are created.
D) It is derived from simah, so names are eternal.

Key Takeaway
'Eternity' in this context means pre-creation placement, and the Sunni view is that 'ism' derives from sumuww, implying Allah eternally has names.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You say you believe Allah's names are eternal—but do you live as if they are merely labels you learned, or as if they reveal His very nature? What would change if you truly took the 'sumuww' etymology to heart?
?
If someone asked you today, 'Are Allah's names created or eternal?'—could you explain the nuance that 'eternal' here means placed before creation, not uncreated in the absolute sense? If not, what does that say about how deeply you have engaged with this lesson?

The First Two Answers and Their Refutations
Presentation and critique of two answers on the pre-eternity of divine names.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the initial attempts to explain the pre-eternity of divine names and why they fail to refute the Mu'tazila.

Core Idea
Pre-eternity as suitability or as knowledge fails to address the Mu'tazila claim that names are human inventions.

The Mu'tazila argue that divine names are created because they are human inventions. Scholars propose various meanings for the pre-eternity of names. In this passage, the author presents two early answers—pre-eternity through 'suitability' and through divine knowledge—and shows their insufficiency. You will see why these answers do not satisfy the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic text for this lesson, focusing on the first two answers. Notice the structure: first the suitability answer, then its critique, then the knowledge answer, then its critique.
2
Identify the two answers
The passage gives two distinct ways to argue that the names are pre-eternal: (a) because Allah is suitable for them, and (b) because of His eternal knowledge. Write down the reasoning for each.
3
Understand the critique
For each answer, the author points out a flaw. The suitability answer does not refute the Mu'tazila because it does not contradict their claim. The knowledge answer applies to all contingent things, so it doesn't distinguish the names. Summarize these critiques.
4
Reflect on the challenge
Why is it difficult to prove that the names themselves (as words) are pre-eternal? The Mu'tazila view them as human products. These answers try to shift the meaning of pre-eternity. Consider what alternative approaches might work.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأجيب بأن معنى قدمها : أن الله صالح لها أزلاً، فهي قديمة باعتبار الصلاحية. وفيه أن هذا لا يحسن في الرد على المعتزلة الذين يقولون : إنها من وضع الخلق، إذ لا ينافيه وبعضهم أجاب بأن قدمها من حيث علم الله تعالى وتقديره في الأزل، وفيه أن جميع الحوادث كذلك، وقيل: إن قدمها من حيث مدلولها ، وفيه أن قدم المدلول يرجع لما سبق من قدم الذات والصفات ولا يحسن في الرد على المعتزلة ونقل العلامة الملوي عن سيدي محمد بن عبد الله العربي : فيما سبق أن من كلام الله القديم أسماء له هي المحكوم عليها بالقدم، كما أن منه أمراً ونهياً ... إلى آخره)، وعلى هذا فالمراد بالتسمية القديمة دلالة الكلام أزلاً على معاني الأسماء من غير تبعيض ولا تجزئة في الكلام، وهو الذي ينشرح له الصدر، ولا يرد أنهم لم يذكروا من أقسام الكلام الاعتبارية الأسماء القديمة؛ لأن تقسيمهم ليس حاصراً، بل اقتصروا على الأهم باعتبار ما ظهر لهم كيف ومدلوله لا يدخل تحت حصر؟ الأولية وأشار العلامة الملوي في آخر عبارته إلى أن القدم هنا ليس بمعنى عدم بل بمعنى أنها موضوعة قبل الخلق، فهي من وضعه تعالى قبل خلقه، ثم ألهمها للنور المحمدي، ثم للملائكة، ثم للخلق، خلافاً للمعتزلة في قولهم : بأنها من وضع البشر، وفي هذا الكلام تسليم أن الأسماء ليست أزلية كما لا يخفى. وبالجملة : فهذا المبحث لم يصف
And it was answered that the meaning of its pre-eternity is that Allah is suitable for it eternally, so it is pre-eternal in consideration of suitability. But there is in this that it does not improve in refuting the Mu'tazila who say that they are from the creation of the creatures, since it does not contradict it. And some of them answered that its pre-eternity is from the perspective of Allah's knowledge and decree in pre-eternity, but there is in this that all contingent things are likewise. And it was said: its pre-eternity is from the perspective of its signification, but there is in this that the pre-eternity of the signification returns to what preceded of the pre-eternity of the Essence and attributes, and it does not improve in refuting the Mu'tazila. And al-ʿAllāma al-Mallawī transmitted from Sayyidī Muḥammad ibn ʿAbd Allāh al-ʿArabī: concerning what preceded, that from the pre-eternal speech of Allah are names for Him that are judged to be pre-eternal, just as from it are command and prohibition ... to the end. And upon this, what is intended by the eternal naming is the eternal indication of speech upon the meanings of the names without division or fragmentation in speech, and this is what the heart opens to. And it is not objected that they did not mention from the notional divisions of speech the pre-eternal names, because their division is not exhaustive; rather they confined themselves to the most important in consideration of what appeared to them, how, and its signification is not encompassable?! The primacy: And al-ʿAllāma al-Mallawī indicated at the end of his expression that pre-eternity here is not in the sense of non-existence but in the sense that they are placed before creation, so they are from His placing, exalted, before His creation, then He inspired them to the Muḥammadan light, then to the angels, then to the creation, contrary to the Muʿtazila in their saying that they are from the placement of humans, and in this speech there is an admission that the names are not eternal, as is not hidden. And in summary: this topic has not become clear.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage is a set of responses to the objection that the divine names are created. It begins by presenting an answer: the pre-eternity of the names means that Allah is eternally suitable for them, so they are pre-eternal in terms of suitability. However, this answer is criticized as insufficient against the Mu'tazila who claim the names are human inventions. A second answer says their pre-eternity is from Allah's eternal knowledge and decree, but this applies to all contingent things. A third says it is from their signification, but this reduces to the pre-eternity of the Essence and attributes. The author then transmits a better answer from al-Mallawi: from Allah's eternal speech are names judged pre-eternal, just as commands and prohibitions are. Thus, the eternal naming means that speech eternally indicates the meanings of the names without division. This is the preferred answer. Al-Mallawi further clarifies that pre-eternity here means the names were placed by Allah before creation and then inspired to the Muhammadan light, angels, and creation, contrary to the Mu'tazila. The author concludes that this topic is not settled.

Key Terms
قدم (qidam)
صلاحية (salāhiyya)
أزل (azal)
معتزلة (Mu'tazila)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The pre-eternity of divine names can be established by saying Allah is suitable for them or He knew them eternally.
Correction: These explanations are not refutations of the Mu'tazila claim that the names are human inventions; they avoid the issue or apply to all contingent things.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the problem with the first answer that Allah's suitability makes the names pre-eternal?
A) It denies that names are created.
B) It does not contradict the Mu'tazila claim that names are human inventions.
C) It makes all creation pre-eternal.
D) It is not based on the Quran.

Key Takeaway
The early answers to the Mu'tazila objection fail because they shift the meaning of pre-eternity without directly addressing the claim that names are human inventions.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever settled for a theological explanation that felt tidy but left you with a lingering unease—like the answers in this passage? What was it, and why did you accept it?
?
The Mu'tazila saw divine names as human inventions. In your own life, do you ever reduce God's attributes—like His mercy or justice—to mere concepts you can define, rather than realities that define you?
?
When you face a doubt about your faith, do you brush it away with a ready answer, or do you wrestle until the truth truly settles in your heart? Which of these two answers would you have been tempted to give before reading this refutation?

The Third Answer: Pre-Eternity Through Signification
Presentation and critique of the third answer based on signification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the third answer that the names are pre-eternal because their signification (the meanings they refer to) is pre-eternal, and why this answer also fails.

Core Idea
Pre-eternity of signification reduces to pre-eternity of attributes, not names.

After two insufficient answers, a third proposal is presented: the divine names are pre-eternal because their signification (what they refer to) is pre-eternal. However, this answer faces the problem that the pre-eternity of the signification is nothing more than the pre-eternity of Allah's Essence and attributes, which does not directly address the pre-eternity of the names themselves. The author also notes it fails to refute the Mu'tazila.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic excerpt carefully
Read the Arabic excerpt, identifying the third answer and its critique.
2
Understand the reasoning
The names are pre-eternal because they signify pre-eternal meanings (e.g., Allah's attributes are pre-eternal). Therefore, the names are pre-eternal in terms of what they indicate.
3
Grasp the critique
The pre-eternity of the signification is derivative of the pre-eternity of the Essence and attributes, so it does not establish a unique pre-eternity for the names as words or as designations. Moreover, it does not refute the Mu'tazila.
4
Compare with previous answers
How is this answer different from the first two? All three share the same pattern of redefining pre-eternity to avoid the issue.

The Preferred Answer: Eternal Indication in Divine Speech
Transmission of al-Mallawi's preferred answer and clarification of qidam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the preferred resolution to the Mu'tazila objection: that the divine names are pre-eternal because Allah's speech eternally indicates their meanings without division, and that this pre-eternity refers to their being placed by Allah before creation.

Core Idea
Divine names are pre-eternal as eternal indications in Allah's speech, placed by Him before creation.

After reviewing and rejecting three earlier answers, the author transmits a more satisfactory solution from al-ʿAllāma al-Mallawī. This answer grounds the pre-eternity of the names in Allah's eternal speech: His speech eternally indicates the meanings of the names without fragmentation. Furthermore, al-Mallawī clarifies that 'pre-eternity' here does not mean absolute non-existence but rather that the names were placed by Allah before creation and then inspired to the Muhammadan light, angels, and humans. This directly counters the Mu'tazila claim that names are human inventions.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage
Read the Arabic passage for this lesson, noting the key phrases: 'من كلام الله القديم أسماء له', 'دلالة الكلام أزلاً على معاني الأسماء', 'القدم هنا ليس بمعنى عدم'.
2
Understand the preferred answer
The names are part of Allah's pre-eternal speech, and the eternal naming means that speech eternally indicates those meanings without division.
3
Grasp the clarification of qidam
Pre-eternity does not mean the names existed as separate words from eternity but rather that Allah placed them before creation and then inspired them.
4
See how this refutes the Mu'tazila
If names are from Allah's placement, they are not human inventions.
5
Note the final concession
The author admits that this topic has not become clear (lam yasfu), indicating some remaining ambiguity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have learned that Allah's names are not human inventions but eternal indications in His speech. Do you call upon Him by His names with the awe that they are from His very speech, or do you recite them as mere words? How will you change your invocation today?
?
The author admits this topic remains unclear. Does your faith require complete intellectual clarity, or can you trust Allah's wisdom even in matters that are not fully understood? What is one area where you need to surrender your need for certainty?
?
Al-Mallawi says this understanding is what 'the heart opens to'. When you ponder the names of Allah, does your heart open to their eternal meanings, or has familiarity made you numb? What one name will you meditate on deeply this week to reawaken your heart?

Two Etymologies of 'Ism' and Their Theological Impact
Classification of two views on the derivation of 'ism' and their implications.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the two different etymologies of the word 'ism' (name) lead to opposing theological conclusions about the pre-eternity of Allah's names and attributes.

Core Idea
Two roots for 'ism' lead to two schools: Sunni eternal names vs. Mu'tazila created names.

This passage from al-Jawhara al-Tawhid delves into a seemingly linguistic issue—the derivation of the word 'ism'—but reveals a deep theological divide. Al-Qurtubi presents two views: one from Ahl al-Sunna and one from the Mu'tazila, each rooted in a different etymology. The implications touch on whether Allah's names are eternal or created, a dispute so severe that one scholar deemed it worse than the claim that the Qur'an is created.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic source once, then the translation. Notice the structure: two views presented with their etymological bases.
Pay attention to the strong condemnation at the end.
2
Analyze each view
For each view, identify the etymology and the resulting theological position about Allah's names.
The first view leads to eternal names; the second to created names.
3
Consider the condemnation
Note al-Shumnunni's strong statement calling the second view worse than the claim that the Qur'an is created. Reflect on why.
This shows the gravity of the debate in Sunni theology.
4
Reflect on broader significance
Understand that this debate is not merely linguistic but concerns the nature of Allah and His attributes.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ونقل عن القرطبي : أن من قال: الاسم مشتق من السمو وهو العلو، يقول: لم يزل الله موصوفاً قبل وجود الخلق وعند وجودهم وبعد فنائهم لا تأثير لهم أسمائه، وهذا قول أهل السنة. ومن قال : الاسم مشتق من السمة، يقول: كان في الأزل بلا أسماء ولا صفات، فلما خلق الخلق جعلوها له، وبعد فنائهم يبقى بدونها، وهو قول المعتزلة، قال الشمني : وهو أقبح من القول بخلق القرآن. انتهى. أفاده العلامة الأمير مع بعض زيادة.
And it was transmitted from al-Qurṭubī: that whoever says that 'ism' is derived from 'al-sumuww' which is height, says: Allah has never ceased to be described [by His names] before the existence of creatures, at their existence, and after their perishing; their names have no effect on Him. And this is the statement of Ahl al-Sunna. And whoever says that 'ism' is derived from 'al-sima' (mark), says: He was in pre-eternity without names and attributes; when He created creatures, they made them for Him, and after their perishing He remains without them. And this is the statement of the Muʿtazila. Al-Shumnunni said: It is uglier than the statement that the Qur'an is created. End. The noble al-Amir conveyed it with some addition.
Source pages p. 203

Commentary
This passage presents a classification of two opposing theological views based on the etymology of the word 'ism' (name). The first view derives 'ism' from 'al-sumuww' (height, exaltation), and concludes that Allah has always been described by His names, independent of creation; this is the Sunni position. The second view derives 'ism' from 'al-sima' (mark, sign), and concludes that names were created by creatures for Allah after creation; this is the Mu'tazila position. The author adds a harsh condemnation from al-Shumnunni, calling this view worse than the belief in the createdness of the Qur'an, highlighting the gravity of the debate.

Correction: This passage demonstrates that the derivation of 'ism' directly impacts the doctrine of divine names and attributes, dividing Sunni and Mu'tazila.

The derivation of the word 'ism' is not merely a grammatical question but a theological battleground with direct implications for the pre-eternity of Allah's names.

The Superiority of God's Names: Are They Equal or Varying in Excellence?
Debate on equality vs. superiority of divine names, settled in favor of superiority.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the theological debate on whether God's names are equal or vary in excellence, and know the correct view that they are superior with 'Allah' being the greatest.

Core Idea
God's names are not equal; the name 'Allah' is the greatest of them all.

This passage from Tuhfat al-Mureed addresses a subtle question: Do the divine names all share the same rank, or are some more excellent than others? We encounter two positions—one asserting equality, the other superiority—and the author settles the debate by affirming the superiority of the name 'Allah'. Let's read the passage and follow the argument.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the two views
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation. Notice that the author first presents the view that all names are equal, then the view that they are superior.
The equality view is attributed to Ibn al-Arabi.
2
Understand the reasoning behind each view
Ibn al-Arabi argues that names refer to the same Essence, so any superiority is external. The correct view asserts intrinsic superiority, with 'Allah' as the greatest name.
The Quranic verses cited by Ali Wafa support the superiority view.
3
Consider the evidence from Quranic verses
Ali Wafa interprets Q. 9:40 and Q. 29:45 to show that the name 'Allah' is highest and its remembrance is greatest.
These verses are direct proof for the superiority of the name 'Allah'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التفاضل بين أسماء الله تعالى واختلف هل بينها تفاضل أو لا؟ فقيل: لا تفاضل بينها، وفي اليواقيت عن ابن العربي: أن أسماء الله تعالى متساوية في نفس الأمر؛ لرجوعها كلها إلى ذات واحدة، وإن وقع فيها تفاضل فإن ذلك لأمر خارج، والحق: أنها متفاضلة وأعظمها لفظ الجلالة؛ وهو الاسم الأعظم، وكان سيدي علي وفا رضي الله عنه يذهب إلى التفاضل في الأسماء، ويقول في قوله تعالى: ﴿وَكَلِمَةُ اللَّهِ هِيَ الْعُليا﴾ [التوبة: 40]: هي اسم الله؛ فإنه أعلى مرتبة من سائر الأسماء، قال: ونظير ذلك قوله تعالى: ﴿وَلَذِكْرُ اللَّهِ أَكْبَرُ﴾ [العنكبوت: 45]؛ أي: ولذكر اسم الله أكبر من ذكر سائر الأسماء. انتهى أفاده الشيخ الأمير.
The superiority among the names of God, may He be exalted. And they differed: is there superiority among them or not? It was said: there is no superiority among them. And in 'al-Yawaqit' from Ibn al-Arabi: that the names of God are equal in reality, because they all refer to one Essence, and if superiority occurs among them, it is for an external reason. And the truth is: that they are superior, and the greatest of them is the word of majesty (al-Jalalah); it is the greatest name. And my master Ali Wafa, may God be pleased with him, went to the superiority among the names, and he said regarding the saying of God the Exalted: 'And the word of Allah is the highest' (Quran 9:40): it is the name of Allah, for it is higher in rank than all other names. He said: and the parallel to that is the saying of God the Exalted: 'And the remembrance of Allah is greatest' (Quran 29:45); i.e., the remembrance of the name of Allah is greater than the remembrance of all other names. End. This was conveyed by al-Shaykh al-Amir.
Source pages p. 204

Commentary
This passage presents a theological debate on whether God's names differ in merit. Two positions are cited: first, the view of Ibn al-Arabi (from the work 'al-Yawaqit') that all names are equal since they all refer to the same Essence; any apparent superiority is due to an external factor (such as the effect of the name on the one who recites it). Second, the correct view (al-Haqq) is that the names are indeed superior one to another, with the name 'Allah' (Al-Lafz al-Jalalah) being the greatest name (al-Ism al-A'zam). This is supported by the authority of Sayyidi Ali Wafa, who interprets two Quranic verses: Q. 9:40 'the word of Allah is the highest' he explains as referring to the name 'Allah', and Q. 29:45 'the remembrance of Allah is greatest' he interprets as the remembrance of the name 'Allah' being greater than the remembrance of any other name. The passage closes by attributing this report to al-Shaykh al-Amir.

الاسم الأعظم
لفظ الجلالة
ابن العربي
علي وفا

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All divine names are equal in merit.
Correction: The correct view is that the names are superior, with 'Allah' being the greatest name.

The Eternity of God's Essential Attributes
Proves the eternity of essential attributes via reduction to absurdity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the essential attributes of God must be eternal and what would be impossible if they were originated.

Core Idea
God's essential attributes are eternal because origination would contradict His absolute self-sufficiency.

This passage from Sharh al-Jawharah establishes a foundational principle in Islamic theology: the attributes that subsist in God's essence (sifat al-dhat) are eternal. The author argues that if they were not eternal, it would lead to logical impossibilities that contradict God's necessary absolute self-sufficiency. This lesson unpacks that proof.

Guided Path
1
Introduce the statement
The commentator begins by explaining the poet's line: 'Likewise the attributes of His Essence are eternal.' This includes the seven (or eight) attributes of meanings.
These are the essential attributes: knowledge, power, will, etc.
2
Understand the proof
The proof is a reduction: if attributes were originated, it would force three impossibilities regarding God's essence and His self-sufficiency. Identify each impossibility.
The proof is based on the concept of al-ghina al-mutlaq (absolute self-sufficiency).
3
Contrast absolute vs restricted self-sufficiency
Note the distinction between God's absolute self-sufficiency and the restricted self-sufficiency of creatures. This reinforces the argument.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قدم صفات الذات قوله: (كَذَا صِفَاتُ ذَاتِهِ قَدِيمَة) أي: مثل أسمائه تعالى الصفات القائمة بذاته، وهي صفات المعاني السبع أو الثمان على الخلاف في ذلك قديمة، فكل من أسمائه وصفات ذاته قديم، فليست أسماؤه من وضع خلقه له وليست صفاته حادثة؛ لأنها لو كانت حادثة لزم قيام الحوادث بذاته تعالى ويلزم كونه تعالى عارياً عنها في الأزل، ويلزم افتقارها إلى مخصص، وهو ينافي وجوب الغنى المطلق، وهو انتفاء الحاجات مطلقاً، وهو لا يكون إلا الله، بخلاف الغنى المقيد، وهو قلة الحاجات، وهو غنى الحوادث، ولذلك قال بعضهم: مولانا؛ غناك مطلق، وغنانا مقيد. وخرج بإضافة صفات إلى الذات صفات الأفعال، فليس شيء منها بقديم عند الأشاعرة، بخلافه عند الماتريدية؛ أي: ولذلك قال صاحب المتن بدء الأمالي ما نصه: صفات الذات والأفعال طرًا قديمات... إلخ وهو موضوع على مذهب الماتريدية؛ لأنها عند الأشاعرة تعلقات التنجيزية الحادثة، وعند الماتريدية هي عين صفة التكوين القديمة كما تقدم.
The eternity of the attributes of the Essence. His saying: 'likewise the attributes of His Essence are eternal': i.e., like His names, may He be exalted, the attributes subsisting in His Essence, which are the seven or eight attributes of meanings, according to the difference on that, are eternal. So each of His names and attributes of His Essence is eternal. Therefore His names are not from the invention of His creation, nor are His attributes originated. Because if they were originated, it would necessitate the occurrence of originated things in His Essence, may He be exalted, and it would necessitate that He, may He be exalted, was devoid of them in pre-eternity, and it would necessitate their need for a specifier, and that contradicts the necessity of absolute self-sufficiency, which is the absence of needs absolutely, and that is only for God, unlike restricted self-sufficiency, which is fewness of needs, and that is the self-sufficiency of originated things. Therefore some said: 'Our Master: Your self-sufficiency is absolute, and our self-sufficiency is restricted.' And by adding 'attributes' to 'Essence', the attributes of actions are excluded, so none of them is eternal according to the Ash'aris, contrary to the Maturidis. That is why the author of the poem 'Bad' al-Amali' said: 'The attributes of the Essence and of actions all are eternal... etc.' And it is built on the Maturidi school, because for the Ash'aris they are originated contingent relations, and for the Maturidis they are the very attribute of origination (takwin) which is eternal, as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a proof and classification: it proves the eternity of God's essential attributes (sifat al-dhat) and distinguishes them from the attributes of action (sifat al-af'al). The commentator explains that the poet's line 'likewise the attributes of His Essence are eternal' means that the attributes subsisting in God's Essence—the seven (or eight, depending on the view) attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani)—are eternal. All of God's names and essential attributes are eternal, not invented by creation. The proof follows: if they were originated, several impossibilities would follow: (1) originated things would subsist in God; (2) God would have been devoid of them in pre-eternity; (3) they would require a specifier. All this contradicts God's necessary absolute self-sufficiency (al-ghina al-mutlaq), which is the absence of needs. The commentator adds a poetic quote contrasting God's absolute self-sufficiency with the restricted self-sufficiency of creatures. Then the passage clarifies that the phrase 'attributes of His Essence' excludes the attributes of action (like creating, giving life, etc.), which are not eternal according to the Ash'aris—unlike the Maturidis. The Maturidi view is referenced via the poem 'Bad' al-Amali' which declares all attributes eternal. The commentator explains that Ash'aris consider attributes of action as originated contingent relations (ta'aluqat tajiziyya haditha), whereas Maturidis consider them identical to the eternal attribute of origination (takwin).

Key Terms
صفات الذات
صفات المعاني
الغنى المطلق
الغنى المقيد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that the essential attributes of God could be created or originated.
Correction: The passage proves that origination of essential attributes leads to contradictions with God's self-sufficiency, so they must be eternal.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what would be a consequence if the essential attributes were originated?
A) They would be eternal.
B) They would require a specifier.
C) God would become more perfect.
D) They would be identical to God's essence.

Key Takeaway
The essential attributes of God are eternal because origination would undermine His absolute self-sufficiency.

If God's essential attributes are eternal, His justice, mercy, and love have always been exactly as they are now. Does this comfort you or unsettle you? How will you adjust your expectations of God's treatment of you based on this truth?

Attributes of Essence vs. Attributes of Action (Ash'ari vs. Maturidi)
Distinguishes attributes of action from essential attributes and contrasts Ash'ari and Maturidi views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the distinction between attributes of essence and attributes of action, and the difference between Ash'ari and Maturidi views on the eternity of the latter.

Core Idea
The attributes of action are not eternal for Ash'aris, but are eternal for Maturidis.

Having established the eternity of the essential attributes, the passage now distinguishes them from the attributes of action (sifat al-af'al), such as creating and giving life. A significant sectarian difference is introduced: while the Ash'aris hold that these attributes are originated contingent relations, the Maturidis consider them identical to the eternal attribute of origination (takwin). This lesson clarifies that distinction.

Guided Path
1
Examine the exclusion
The phrase 'attributes of His Essence' excludes the attributes of action. Understand why they are not included in the eternity claim.
2
Understand the Ash'ari position
For Ash'aris, attributes of action are not eternal; they are originated contingent relations (ta'aluqat tajiziyya haditha). These are not eternal, but are new relations that occur in time.
This view emphasizes that God's actions are temporally linked to His will.
3
Understand the Maturidi position
For Maturidis, attributes of action are identical to the attribute of takwin (origination), which is eternal. Thus they say all attributes are eternal.
The poem 'Bad' al-Amali' is cited to illustrate this view.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قدم صفات الذات قوله: (كَذَا صِفَاتُ ذَاتِهِ قَدِيمَة) أي: مثل أسمائه تعالى الصفات القائمة بذاته، وهي صفات المعاني السبع أو الثمان على الخلاف في ذلك قديمة، فكل من أسمائه وصفات ذاته قديم، فليست أسماؤه من وضع خلقه له وليست صفاته حادثة؛ لأنها لو كانت حادثة لزم قيام الحوادث بذاته تعالى ويلزم كونه تعالى عارياً عنها في الأزل، ويلزم افتقارها إلى مخصص، وهو ينافي وجوب الغنى المطلق، وهو انتفاء الحاجات مطلقاً، وهو لا يكون إلا الله، بخلاف الغنى المقيد، وهو قلة الحاجات، وهو غنى الحوادث، ولذلك قال بعضهم: مولانا؛ غناك مطلق، وغنانا مقيد. وخرج بإضافة صفات إلى الذات صفات الأفعال، فليس شيء منها بقديم عند الأشاعرة، بخلافه عند الماتريدية؛ أي: ولذلك قال صاحب المتن بدء الأمالي ما نصه: صفات الذات والأفعال طرًا قديمات... إلخ وهو موضوع على مذهب الماتريدية؛ لأنها عند الأشاعرة تعلقات التنجيزية الحادثة، وعند الماتريدية هي عين صفة التكوين القديمة كما تقدم.
The eternity of the attributes of the Essence. His saying: 'likewise the attributes of His Essence are eternal': i.e., like His names, may He be exalted, the attributes subsisting in His Essence, which are the seven or eight attributes of meanings, according to the difference on that, are eternal. So each of His names and attributes of His Essence is eternal. Therefore His names are not from the invention of His creation, nor are His attributes originated. Because if they were originated, it would necessitate the occurrence of originated things in His Essence, may He be exalted, and it would necessitate that He, may He be exalted, was devoid of them in pre-eternity, and it would necessitate their need for a specifier, and that contradicts the necessity of absolute self-sufficiency, which is the absence of needs absolutely, and that is only for God, unlike restricted self-sufficiency, which is fewness of needs, and that is the self-sufficiency of originated things. Therefore some said: 'Our Master: Your self-sufficiency is absolute, and our self-sufficiency is restricted.' And by adding 'attributes' to 'Essence', the attributes of actions are excluded, so none of them is eternal according to the Ash'aris, contrary to the Maturidis. That is why the author of the poem 'Bad' al-Amali' said: 'The attributes of the Essence and of actions all are eternal... etc.' And it is built on the Maturidi school, because for the Ash'aris they are originated contingent relations, and for the Maturidis they are the very attribute of origination (takwin) which is eternal, as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a proof and classification: it proves the eternity of God's essential attributes (sifat al-dhat) and distinguishes them from the attributes of action (sifat al-af'al). The commentator explains that the poet's line 'likewise the attributes of His Essence are eternal' means that the attributes subsisting in God's Essence—the seven (or eight, depending on the view) attributes of meanings (sifat al-ma'ani)—are eternal. All of God's names and essential attributes are eternal, not invented by creation. The proof follows: if they were originated, several impossibilities would follow: (1) originated things would subsist in God; (2) God would have been devoid of them in pre-eternity; (3) they would require a specifier. All this contradicts God's necessary absolute self-sufficiency (al-ghina al-mutlaq), which is the absence of needs. The commentator adds a poetic quote contrasting God's absolute self-sufficiency with the restricted self-sufficiency of creatures. Then the passage clarifies that the phrase 'attributes of His Essence' excludes the attributes of action (like creating, giving life, etc.), which are not eternal according to the Ash'aris—unlike the Maturidis. The Maturidi view is referenced via the poem 'Bad' al-Amali' which declares all attributes eternal. The commentator explains that Ash'aris consider attributes of action as originated contingent relations (ta'aluqat tajiziyya haditha), whereas Maturidis consider them identical to the eternal attribute of origination (takwin).

Key Terms
صفات الأفعال
التنجيزية
التكوين
الأشاعرة
الماتريدية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that all attributes of God are eternal in the same way, without distinguishing between essential and action attributes.
Correction: While essential attributes are eternal, the attributes of action are considered created by Ash'aris, but eternal by Maturidis.

Checkpoint
According to the Ash'ari view mentioned in the passage, what are attributes of action?
A) Eternal attributes of God.
B) Originated contingent relations.
C) Identical to the essence of God.
D) Nonexistent.

Key Takeaway
The distinction between essential and action attributes is a major point of difference between Ash'aris and Maturidis regarding eternity.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you witness a blessing in your life, do you see it as a fresh act of God or as a manifestation of His eternal attribute of origination? How does that perspective affect your gratitude?
?
If God's actions are not eternal (as Ash'aris hold), does that make you appreciate each moment as a new, direct intervention from Him? Or does it create uncertainty about His constancy? Examine your heart: which view brings you closer to trusting Him?

Divine Attributes

The Pre-existence of God's Essential Attributes
The commentator proves the pre-existence of attributes of essence and distinguishes them from attributes of action.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the rational proof that God's essential attributes are pre-existent and how they differ from attributes of action in Ash'ari theology.

Core Idea
God's essential attributes must be eternal because their temporality would contradict His absolute self-sufficiency.

This passage from Tuhfat al-Mureed provides a clear argument for why the attributes that subsist in God's essence must be pre-existent. The commentator uses a proof by contradiction to show that if these attributes were created, it would lead to impossible consequences that violate God's perfection. He then distinguishes these eternal attributes from the attributes of action, which are temporal according to the Ash'aris.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage once to get a sense of its flow. Then read the literal translation carefully, noting the key terms.
Pay attention to the distinction between 'صفات الذات' and 'صفات الأفعال'.
2
Identify the definition of attributes of essence
Notice how the commentator defines attributes of essence as the seven or eight eternal attributes subsisting in God's essence, and states that they are pre-existent.
He also mentions God's names are not invented by creation.
3
Trace the proof by contradiction
The commentator presents a proof: if attributes were temporal (حادثة), three impossible consequences follow. List each consequence and see how they contradict God's perfection.
The three consequences are: (1) temporal events in God's essence, (2) God devoid of attributes in pre-eternity, (3) need for a particulariser.
4
Understand absolute vs. restricted self-sufficiency
The proof concludes that temporal attributes would need a particulariser, which contradicts God's absolute self-sufficiency (الغنى المطلق). Contrast this with restricted self-sufficiency of creatures.
This is a key argument in kalām for the eternity of attributes.
5
Learn the distinction from attributes of action
The passage then excludes attributes of action from being pre-existent according to Ash'aris, and explains the Maturidi view that they are identical to the eternal attribute of origination.
This highlights a school difference within Sunni theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (كَذَا صِفَاتُ ذَاتِهِ قَدِيمَة) أي : مثل أسمائه تعالى الصفات القائمة بذاته، وهي صفات المعاني السبع أو الثمان على الخلاف في ذلك قديمة، فكل من أسمائه وصفات ذاته قديم، فليست أسماؤه من وضع خلقه له وليست صفاته حادثة؛ لأنَّها لو كانت حادثةٌ لزم قيام الحوادث بذاته تعالى ويلزم كونه تعالى عارياً عنها في الأزل، ويلزم افتقارها إلى مخصص، وهو ينافي وجوب الغنى المطلق، وهو انتفاء الحاجات مطلقاً، وهو لا يكون إلا الله، بخلاف الغنى المقيَّدِ، وهو قِلَّةُ الحاجات، وهو غنى الحوادث، ولذلك قال بعضهم : مولانا غناك مطلق، وغنانا مقيّد .
وخرج بإضافة صفات إلى الذات صفاتُ الأفعال، فليس شيء منها بقديم عند الأشاعرة، بخلافه عند الماتريدية؛ أي: ولذلك قال صاحب المتن بدء الأمالي ما نصه : صِفَاتُ الذَّاتِ والأفعالِ طُرًا قَدِيمات ... إلخ
وهو موضوع على مذهب الماتريدية؛ لأنَّها عند الأشاعرة تعلقات التنجيزية الحادثة، وعند الماتريدية هي عين صفة التكوين القديمة كما تقدَّم.
His statement: (Likewise the attributes of His essence are pre-existent) meaning: like His names, may He be exalted, the attributes subsisting in His essence, and they are the seven or eight attributes of meanings, according to the difference of opinion in that, are pre-existent. So each of His names and the attributes of His essence is pre-existent. Thus His names are not from the invention of His creation, nor are His attributes created; because if they were created, it would necessitate the occurrence of temporal events in His essence, may He be exalted, and it would necessitate that He, may He be exalted, be devoid of them in pre-eternity, and it would necessitate their need for a particulariser, which contradicts the necessity of absolute self-sufficiency, which is the negation of needs absolutely, and that belongs only to God, contrary to restricted self-sufficiency, which is fewness of needs, and that is the self-sufficiency of temporal beings. And therefore some said: Our Master, Your self-sufficiency is absolute, and our self-sufficiency is restricted.
And by adding 'attributes' to 'essence', the attributes of actions are excluded, so none of them is pre-existent according to the Ash'aris, contrary to the Maturidis. That is, therefore the author of Bad' al-Amali said the text: 'The attributes of essence and actions all are pre-existent... etc.' And it is based on the Maturidi school, because they are, according to the Ash'aris, contingent relative determinations, and according to the Maturidis, they are identical to the attribute of origination which is pre-existent, as previously mentioned.
Source pages p. 205

Commentary
This passage is a definition and proof. The commentator first defines the attributes of essence (ṣifāt al-dhāt) as the seven or eight eternal attributes that subsist in God's essence. He states that both names and attributes of essence are pre-existent (qadīm). Then he provides a proof by contradiction: if the attributes were temporal (ḥāditha), it would lead to three impossibilities: (1) temporal events would occur in God's essence, (2) God would have been devoid of them in pre-eternity, and (3) they would need a particulariser (mukhaṣṣiṣ), all of which contradict God's absolute self-sufficiency (ghinā muṭlaq). He contrasts absolute self-sufficiency, which belongs only to God, with restricted self-sufficiency of created beings. Then he distinguishes attributes of action (ṣifāt al-fi'l), which are not pre-existent according to the Ash'aris, but according to the Maturidis they are pre-existent as they are identical to the attribute of origination (takwīn). The commentary on the poem affirms compatibility with the Maturidi view by citing Bad' al-Amali.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that all attributes of God, including attributes of action, are equally pre-existent.
Correction: Ash'aris distinguish between attributes of essence (eternal) and attributes of action (temporal), while Maturidis consider attributes of action to be identical to the eternal attribute of origination.

Checkpoint
What is the core argument that the attributes of God's essence must be pre-existent?
A) Because they are mentioned in the Qur'an.
B) If they were created, it would necessitate temporal events in God, Him being devoid of them in pre-eternity, and their needing a particulariser, contradicting absolute self-sufficiency.
C) Because the Prophet ﷺ said so.
D) Because the Maturidis said so.

Key Takeaway
God's essential attributes must be pre-existent because their temporality would lead to impossible consequences that contradict His absolute self-sufficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly rely on God's absolute self-sufficiency, or do you still place your trust in your own limited resources and abilities, fearing their failure? Reflect on a moment when you panicked over a need—did you act as if God might not provide?
?
When you pray, do you deeply feel that God's attributes are perfect and eternal—unlike your own fleeting qualities—or do you approach Him as if He were a powerful but limited being? Ask yourself: have I ever doubted His knowledge or power in a situation?
?
The lesson states that if God's attributes were temporal, He would have been devoid of them in pre-eternity. This means God has always been perfect. How does this truth challenge your tendency to think God's help or mercy might 'run out' or be conditional? Commit to one specific way you will remember His eternal perfection during a trial.

Positions on Tawqifiyyah of God's Names and Attributes
Commentator presents majority view and different scholarly opinions on tawqifiyyah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the majority view that God's names and attributes require scriptural authorization and the key differences among scholars.

Core Idea
God's names and attributes must come from revelation — they cannot be invented by reason alone.

This lesson introduces the concept of tawqifiyyah — the idea that God's names and attributes are derived solely from revelation. The commentator surveys various scholarly positions, from the majority insistence on scriptural authorization to the Mu'tazila's allowance of rational affirmation, highlighting the nuance within Sunni thought.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the main positions
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Notice the different views: majority, Mu'tazila, al-Baqillani, al-Juwayni, and al-Ghazali.
The passage is a classic example of ikhtilaf (scholarly disagreement) in kalam.
2
Examine the flow of the argument
Observe how the commentator moves from stating the majority view to mentioning opposing or nuanced views, then concludes with the chosen opinion.
The conclusion (mukhtār) reaffirms the majority stance.
3
Understand the consensus and chosen view
The commentator summarizes that scholars agree on permissibility when revelation permits, impermissibility when it forbids, and differ when silent. The chosen view is prohibition of unscriptural names/attributes.
This shows the importance of adhering to revelation in theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَاخْتِيرَ أَنَّ اسْمَاهُ تَوقِيفِيَّهْ كَذَا الصِّفَاتُ فَاحْفَظ السَّمْعِيَّة
قوله : (وَاخْتِيرَ ... إلخ) أي اختار جمهور أهل السنة أن أسماءه تعالى توقيفية، وكذا صفاته، فلا تُنبِتُ الله اسماً ولا صفةٌ إلا إذا ورد بذلك توقيف من الشارع، وذهبت المعتزلة إلى جواز إثبات ما كان متصفاً بمعناه، ولم يوهم نقصاً، وإن لم يرد به توقيف من الشارع، ومال إليه القاضي أبو بكر الباقلاني، وتوقف فيه إمام الحرمين، وفصل الغزالي فجوّز إطلاق الصفة؛ وهي ما دلّ على معنى زائد على الذات ومنع إطلاق الاسم؛ وهو ما دلّ على نفس الذات.
والحاصل أنَّ علماء الإسلام اتفقوا على جواز إطلاق الأسماء والصفات على الباري عزَّ وجلَّ إذا ورد بها الإذن من الشارع، وعلى امتناعه إذا ورد المنع منه، واختلفوا حيث لا إذنَ ولا منع، والمختار منعُ ذلك، وهو مذهب الجمهور. انتهى. مصنف في شرحه الصغيره.
قوله : (أَنَّ اسْمَاهُ) بدرج همزة أسماءه الأولى مع القصر للوزن، والمراد بالأسماء: ما قابل الصفات، بدليل قوله : (كذا الصفات)، الاسم ما دلّ على الذات، والصفة ما دلّ على معنى زائد على الذات وليس المراد بالاسم ما قابل الفعل والحرف ولا ما قابل الكنية واللقب. (الاسم ما دلّ على الذات) إما وحدها كلفظ الجلالة، وإما مع الصفة كلفظ الرحمن.
And it is chosen that His names are tawqifiyyah, likewise the attributes, so preserve the scriptural [evidence].
His statement: (And it is chosen... etc.) meaning: the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah chose that His names, may He be exalted, are tawqifiyyah, and likewise His attributes, so we do not affirm for God a name nor an attribute unless there has come a tawqif from the Lawgiver. The Mu'tazila went to the permissibility of affirming whatever is descriptive of its meaning, as long as it does not imply deficiency, even if no tawqif from the Lawgiver has come. Al-Qadi Abu Bakr al-Baqillani inclined to that, and Imam al-Haramayn [al-Juwayni] withheld [judgment], and al-Ghazali distinguished: he permitted the application of the attribute, which is what indicates an additional meaning beyond the essence, and forbade the application of the name, which is what indicates the essence itself.
The conclusion is that the scholars of Islam agreed upon the permissibility of applying names and attributes to the Creator, may He be glorified and exalted, when permission from the Lawgiver has come, and upon the impossibility when prohibition has come, and they differed where there is neither permission nor prohibition, and the chosen [view] is the prohibition of that, and that is the school of the majority. End. [The commentator] said in his small commentary.
His statement: (that His names) with elision of the hamza of 'asma'ihi the first, with shortening for meter, and what is meant by names: what is opposed to attributes, evidenced by his statement: (likewise the attributes). The name is what indicates the essence, and the attribute is what indicates an additional meaning beyond the essence. What is meant by name is not what is opposed to verb and particle, nor what is opposed to nickname and honorific. (The name is what indicates the essence) either alone, like the word of majesty, or with the attribute like the word al-Rahman.
Source pages p. 206

Commentary
This passage is a classification of scholarly positions on whether God's names and attributes are tawqifiyyah (dependent on revelation). The commentator begins by quoting the poem line and then explains it. The majority of Ahl al-Sunnah hold that both names and attributes must come from revelation; one cannot invent a name or attribute for God without scriptural authorization. The Mu'tazila allow affirming any name or attribute that corresponds to a meaning that does not imply deficiency, even without revelation. Al-Baqillani inclined to the Mu'tazila position, al-Juwayni suspended judgment, and al-Ghazali distinguished between attributes (which indicate an additional meaning beyond the essence) and names (which indicate the essence itself), permitting the former but not the latter without revelation. The commentator then summarizes a consensus: all agree on permissibility when revelation permits, impermissibility when revelation forbids, and disagreement where revelation is silent; the chosen view (mukhtār) is prohibition, following the majority. Finally, he explains that in the context, 'names' are distinct from 'attributes' and defines both: a name indicates the essence (either alone or with an attribute, e.g., 'Allah' or 'al-Rahman'), while an attribute indicates an additional meaning beyond the essence.

Key Terms
توقيفية
جمهور
معتزلة
الباقلاني
إمام الحرمين
الغزالي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Any name that has a good meaning can be applied to God.
Correction: The majority of Sunni scholars require scriptural authorization (tawqīf) for any name or attribute applied to God.

Checkpoint
According to the majority of Ahl al-Sunnah, what is the condition for applying a name or attribute to God?
A) It must not imply deficiency.
B) It must be based on revelation (tawqif).
C) It must indicate the essence.
D) It must be approved by a consensus.

Key Takeaway
The majority of Ahl al-Sunnah hold that both God's names and attributes are tawqifiyyah, requiring scriptural authorization, and this is the preferred view.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you speak about Allah in your daily conversations, do you ever use descriptions that are not found in the Quran or authentic hadith? What is at stake for your tawhid if you do, and will you commit to only using what the revelation authorizes starting today?
?
The scholars agreed that all names and attributes must be based on revelation, yet they differed on some details. But you are not a scholar making ijtihad—so how can you ensure that your own understanding of Allah is not drawing from your imagination? What specific habit will you adopt to root your knowledge of Allah in the texts?
?
Al-Ghazali permitted attributes without names, but the majority prohibited both. If your heart inclines to a 'reasonable' description of Allah that is not mentioned in revelation, will you submit to the majority's caution or follow your own reasoning? That choice reveals where your true authority lies—what will you choose?
